					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Liquid fat made from olives</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the Popeye character, see <a href="/wiki/Olive_Oyl" title="Olive Oyl">Olive Oyl</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1314755338">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:var(--background-color-interactive-subtle,#f8f9fa);color:inherit;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .infobox .side-box{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1;min-width:0}}@media(min-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style>
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">special characters</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</div></div>
</div>
<p><b>Olive oil</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a> obtained by pressing whole <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olives</a> (the fruit of <i><a href="/wiki/Olea" title="Olea">Olea</a> europaea</i>, a traditional <a href="/wiki/Tree_fruit" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree fruit">tree crop</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>) and extracting the oil.
</p><p>It is commonly used in cooking for frying foods, as a <a href="/wiki/Condiment" title="Condiment">condiment</a>, or as a <a href="/wiki/Salad_dressing" title="Salad dressing">salad dressing</a>. It can also be found in some <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceuticals">pharmaceuticals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soaps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a> for traditional <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamps</a>. It also has additional uses in some religions. The olive is one of three core food plants in <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_cuisine" title="Mediterranean cuisine">Mediterranean cuisine</a>, with wheat and grapes. Olive trees have been cultivated around the Mediterranean since the 8th millennium BC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2022, Spain was the world's largest producer, manufacturing 24% of the world's total. Other large producers were Italy, Greece, and Turkey, collectively accounting for 59% of the global market.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-faostat&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The composition of olive oil varies with the <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>, altitude, time of harvest, and extraction process. It consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a> (up to 83%), with smaller amounts of other <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> including <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a> (up to 21%) and <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic acid</a> (up to 20%). Extra virgin olive oil (EVOO) is required to have no more than 0.8% <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_acidity" title="Olive oil acidity">free acidity</a>, and is considered to have favorable flavor characteristics.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Olive oil has long been a common ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_cuisine" title="Mediterranean cuisine">Mediterranean cuisine</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_cuisine" title="Ancient Greek cuisine">ancient Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_ancient_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuisine of ancient Rome">Roman cuisine</a>. Wild olives, which originated in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, were collected by <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people as early as the 8th millennium BC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davidson,&#95;s.v.&#95;Olives&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson,_s.v._Olives-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Besides food, olive oil has been used for religious <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicines</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soap-making" class="mw-redirect" title="Soap-making">soap-making</a>, and skincare application.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Spartans</a> and other Greeks used oil to rub themselves while exercising in the <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(ancient_Greece)" title="Gymnasium (ancient Greece)">gymnasia</a>. From its beginnings early in the 7th century BC, the cosmetic use of olive oil quickly spread to all of the Hellenic city-states, together with athletes training in the nude, and lasted close to a thousand years despite its great expense.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Olive oil was also popular as a form of birth control; <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Animals" title="History of Animals">History of Animals</a></i> recommends applying a mixture of olive oil combined with either oil of <a href="/wiki/Cedrus" title="Cedrus">cedar</a>, ointment of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, or ointment of <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cervix" title="Cervix">cervix</a> to prevent pregnancy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Early_cultivation">Early cultivation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early cultivation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Klazomenai.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klazomenai.jpg/250px-Klazomenai.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="249" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klazomenai.jpg/330px-Klazomenai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klazomenai.jpg/500px-Klazomenai.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="883" /></a><figcaption>Ancient Greek olive oil production workshop in <a href="/wiki/Klazomenai" title="Klazomenai">Klazomenai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> (modern Turkey)</figcaption></figure>
<p>The exact date and location of olive tree domestication are unclear. The modern olive tree may have originated in ancient Persia and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and spread to the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and later to <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, though some scholars argue for an Egyptian origin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest traces of olive oil in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> date back to the <a href="/wiki/3rd_millennium_BC" title="3rd millennium BC">3rd millennium BC</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The olive tree reached Greece, <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> sometime in the <a href="/wiki/28th_century_BC" title="28th century BC">28th century BC</a>, having been spread westward by the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Until around 1500 BC, eastern coastal areas of the Mediterranean were most heavily cultivated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Evidence also suggests that olives were being grown in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> as long ago as 2500 BC. The earliest surviving olive oil <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphorae</a> date to 3500 BC (<a href="/wiki/Early_Minoan" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Minoan">Early Minoan</a> times), though the production of olive oil is assumed to have started before 4000 BC.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Olive trees were certainly cultivated by the <a href="/wiki/Late_Minoan" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Minoan">Late Minoan</a> period (1500 BC) in Crete, and perhaps as early as the Early Minoan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cultivation of olive trees in Crete became particularly intense in the post-palatial period and played an important role in the island's economy, as it did across the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later, as Greek colonies were established in other parts of the Mediterranean, olive farming was introduced to places like <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and continued to spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Olive trees were introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in the 16th century, when cultivation began in areas with a climate similar to the Mediterranean, such as Chile, Argentina, and <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Recent <a href="/wiki/Genetic_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic studies">genetic studies</a> suggest that species used by modern cultivars descend from multiple wild populations, but detailed history of domestication is unknown.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Trade_and_production">Trade and production</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Trade and production"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Turkey.Bodrum042.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Turkey.Bodrum042.jpg/250px-Turkey.Bodrum042.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Turkey.Bodrum042.jpg/500px-Turkey.Bodrum042.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Ancient oil press (Bodrum Museum of Underwater Archaeology, Bodrum, Turkey)</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archaeological_evidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeological evidence">Archaeological evidence</a> in <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> shows that by 6000 BC olives were being turned into olive oil<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in 4500 BC at a now-submerged prehistoric settlement south of <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Olive trees and oil production in the Eastern Mediterranean can be traced to archives of the ancient city-state <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a> (2600–2240 BC), which were located on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>. Here, some dozen documents dated 2400 BC describe the lands of the king and the queen. These belonged to a library of clay tablets perfectly preserved by having been baked in the fire that destroyed the palace. A later source is the frequent mentions of oil in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ucsc1&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucsc1-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dynastic <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a> before 2000&#160;BC imported olive oil from Crete, Syria, and <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, and oil was an important item of commerce and wealth. Remains of olive oil have been found in jugs over 4,000 years old in a tomb on the island of <a href="/wiki/Naxos" title="Naxos">Naxos</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sinuhe" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinuhe">Sinuhe</a>, the Egyptian exile who lived in northern Canaan c.1960&#160;BC, wrote of abundant olive trees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Minoans used olive oil in religious ceremonies. The oil became a principal product of the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a>, where it is thought to have represented wealth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Olive oil was also a major export of <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a> (c. 1450–1150 BC).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scholars believe the oil was made by a process where olives were placed in <a href="/wiki/Woven_mat" class="mw-disambig" title="Woven mat">woven mats</a> and squeezed. The oil was collected in vats. This process was known from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, was used by the Egyptians, and continued to be used through the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the Iron Age, settlements in and around the <a href="/wiki/Judaean_Foothills" class="mw-redirect" title="Judaean Foothills">Judaean Lowlands</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ekron" title="Ekron">Ekron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timnah" title="Timnah">Timnah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gezer" title="Gezer">Gezer</a>, emerged as key hubs for olive oil production and commerce.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Evidence from the <a href="/wiki/Samaria_Ostraca" title="Samaria Ostraca">Samaria ostraca</a>, discovered in the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Kingdom of Israel</a>, includes a reference to "washed oil," a term believed to refer to virgin olive oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Olive_Press_in_Pompeji.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Olive_Press_in_Pompeji.JPG/250px-Olive_Press_in_Pompeji.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Olive_Press_in_Pompeji.JPG/500px-Olive_Press_in_Pompeji.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Olive crusher <i>(trapetum)</i> in Pompeii (79 AD)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Olive oil may have been produced as early as the Bronze Age in Italy, though this intensified throughout the first millennium BCE and into the Roman period. Some of the earliest evidence of rotary olive mills for the production of olive oil have been found in southern Italy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The importance of olive oil as a commercial commodity increased after the Roman conquest of Egypt, Greece, and Asia Minor, which led to more trade along the Mediterranean. Olive trees were planted throughout the entire Mediterranean basin during the evolution of the Roman Republic and Empire. According to the historian <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, Italy had "excellent olive oil at reasonable prices" by the 1st century AD—"the best in the Mediterranean".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> More sophisticated production techniques such as the <a href="/wiki/Olive_press" class="mw-redirect" title="Olive press">olive press</a> and <i>trapetum</i> (pictured left) became increasingly widespread throughout the Imperial era, and eventually screw presses were commonly used to produce olive oil from Spain to the eastern Mediterranean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many ancient presses still exist in the Eastern Mediterranean region, and some dating to the Roman period are still used today.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ceipac&#95;ub&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceipac_ub-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Productivity was greatly improved by Joseph Graham's development of the <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> pressing system in 1795.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png/250px-The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png" decoding="async" width="190" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png/330px-The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png/500px-The_Manufacture_of_Oil_drawn_and_engraved_by_J_Amman_in_the_Sixteenth_Century.png 2x" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="905" /></a><figcaption><i>The Manufacture of Oil</i>, 16th-century engraving by <a href="/wiki/Jost_Amman" title="Jost Amman">Jost Amman</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Symbolism_and_mythology">Symbolism and mythology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Symbolism and mythology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The olive tree has historically been a symbol of peace between nations. It has played a religious and social role in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, especially concerning the city of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Athens">Athens</a>, named after the goddess <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> because her gift of an olive tree was held to be more precious than rival <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>'s gift of a salt spring.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-review&#95;12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Varieties">Varieties</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Varieties"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_olive_cultivars" title="List of olive cultivars">List of olive cultivars</a></div>
<p>There are many <a href="/wiki/List_of_olive_cultivars" title="List of olive cultivars">olive cultivars</a>, each with a particular flavor, texture, and shelf life that make them more or less suitable for different applications, such as direct human consumption on bread or in salads, indirect consumption in domestic cooking or catering, or industrial uses such as animal feed or engineering applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ucdavis1&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucdavis1-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the stages of maturity, olive fruit changes color from green to violet, and then black. Olive oil taste characteristics depend on the stage of ripeness at which olive fruits are collected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ucdavis1&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucdavis1-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Uses">Uses</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Culinary_use">Culinary use</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Culinary use"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vinegar_%26_Oil.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Vinegar_%26_Oil.JPG/250px-Vinegar_%26_Oil.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Vinegar_%26_Oil.JPG/500px-Vinegar_%26_Oil.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">Vinegar</a> and olive oil</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Artisan_bread_with_olive_oil_and_salt.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Artisan_bread_with_olive_oil_and_salt.jpg/250px-Artisan_bread_with_olive_oil_and_salt.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Artisan_bread_with_olive_oil_and_salt.jpg/500px-Artisan_bread_with_olive_oil_and_salt.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Olive oil served with bread</figcaption></figure>
<p>Olive oil is an important <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">cooking oil</a> in countries surrounding the Mediterranean, and it forms one of the three staple food plants of <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_cuisine" title="Mediterranean cuisine">Mediterranean cuisine</a>, the other two being wheat (as in <a href="/wiki/Pasta" title="Pasta">pasta</a>, bread, and <a href="/wiki/Couscous" title="Couscous">couscous</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>, used as a dessert fruit and for wine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Extra virgin olive oil is mostly used raw as a condiment and as an ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Salad_dressings" class="mw-redirect" title="Salad dressings">salad dressings</a>. If uncompromised by heat, the flavor is stronger. It can also be used for <a href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9ing" title="Sautéing">sautéing</a>.
</p><p>When extra virgin olive oil is heated above 210–216&#160;°C (410–421&#160;°F), depending on its free fatty acid content, the unrefined particles within the oil are burned. This leads to a deteriorated taste. Refined olive oils are suited for <a href="/wiki/Deep_frying" title="Deep frying">deep frying</a> because of the higher <a href="/wiki/Smoke_point" title="Smoke point">smoke point</a> and milder flavour.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extra virgin oils have a smoke point around 180–215&#160;°C (356–419&#160;°F),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sgray&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sgray-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with higher-quality oils having a higher smoke point,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> whereas refined light olive oil has a smoke point up to 230&#160;°C (446&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sgray&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sgray-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That they can be used for deep frying is contrary to the <a href="/wiki/Common_misconception" class="mw-redirect" title="Common misconception">common misconception</a> that there are no olive oils with a smoke point as high as many other <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a>. In the misconception, heating at these high temperatures is theorised to impact taste or nutrition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Religious_use">Religious use</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Religious use"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Christianity">Christianity</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Christianity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Roman Catholic, Eastern Orthodox and Anglican churches use olive oil for the <a href="/wiki/Oil_of_catechumens" title="Oil of catechumens">oil of catechumens</a> (used to bless and strengthen those preparing for baptism) and oil of the sick (used to confer the Sacrament of <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_in_the_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick in the Catholic Church">anointing of the sick</a> or extreme unction). Olive oil mixed with a perfuming agent such as <a href="/wiki/Balsam_of_Mecca" class="mw-redirect" title="Balsam of Mecca">balsam</a> is <a href="/wiki/Consecrated" class="mw-redirect" title="Consecrated">consecrated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> as sacred <a href="/wiki/Chrism" title="Chrism">chrism</a>, which is used to confer the sacrament of <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a> (as a symbol of the strengthening of the Holy Spirit), in the rites of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Orders">ordination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a> and bishops, in the consecration of <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">churches</a>, and, traditionally, in the anointing of monarchs at their <a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronation</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christians</a> still use oil lamps in their churches, home prayer corners, and cemeteries. A vigil lamp consists of a votive glass filled with olive oil, floating on a half-inch of water. The glass has a metal holder that hangs from a bracket on the wall or sits on a table. A cork float with a lit wick floats on the oil. To douse the flame, the float is carefully pressed down into the oil. Makeshift oil lamps can easily be made by soaking a cotton ball in olive oil and forming it into a peak. The peak is lit and then burns until all the oil is consumed, whereupon the rest of the cotton burns out. Olive oil is a usual offering to churches and cemeteries.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> uses virgin olive oil that the priesthood has blessed for anointing the sick.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Judaism">Judaism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Judaism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> observance, olive oil was the only fuel allowed to be used in the seven-branched <a href="/wiki/Menorah_(Temple)" class="mw-redirect" title="Menorah (Temple)">menorah</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mishkan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mishkan">Mishkan</a> service during <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">the Exodus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribes of Israel">Tribes of Israel</a> from <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Egypt" title="Biblical Egypt">Egypt</a>, and later in the permanent <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">Temple in Jerusalem</a>. It was obtained by using only the first drop from a squeezed olive and consecrated for use only in the Temple by the priests and stored in special containers. In modern times, although candles can be used to light the <a href="/wiki/Menorah_(Hanukkah)" class="mw-redirect" title="Menorah (Hanukkah)">menorah at Hanukkah</a>, oil containers are preferred to imitate the original menorah.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Judaism of Ancient Israel, olive oil was also used to prepare the <a href="/wiki/Holy_anointing_oil" title="Holy anointing oil">holy anointing oil</a> used for priests, kings, prophets, and others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other">Other</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Olive oil is also a natural and safe <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricant</a>, and can be used to lubricate kitchen machinery (grinders, blenders, cookware, etc.). It can also be used for illumination (oil lamps) or as the base for soaps and detergents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some cosmetics also use olive oil as their base,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and it can be used as a substitute for machine oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-smith1&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-uidaho1&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uidaho1-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ucanr&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ucanr-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Olive oil has also been used as both solvent and ligand in the synthesis of cadmium selenide <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dots</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ranieri_Filo_della_Torre" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranieri Filo della Torre">Ranieri Filo della Torre</a> is an international literary prize for writings about extra virgin olive oil. It yearly honors poetry, fiction, and non-fiction about extra virgin olive oil.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Extraction">Extraction</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Extraction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cold_press_olive_oil_machine_at_Saba_Habib_in_Israel_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Cold_press_olive_oil_machine_at_Saba_Habib_in_Israel_2.jpg/250px-Cold_press_olive_oil_machine_at_Saba_Habib_in_Israel_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="187" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Cold_press_olive_oil_machine_at_Saba_Habib_in_Israel_2.jpg/500px-Cold_press_olive_oil_machine_at_Saba_Habib_in_Israel_2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3340" data-file-height="2504" /></a><figcaption>A cold press olive oil machine in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Frantoio_Maraldi_2.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Frantoio_Maraldi_2.JPG/250px-Frantoio_Maraldi_2.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Frantoio_Maraldi_2.JPG/500px-Frantoio_Maraldi_2.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption>Olive oil mill</figcaption></figure>
<p>Olive oil is produced by grinding olives and extracting the oil by mechanical or chemical means. Green olives usually produce more bitter oil, and overripe olives can produce oil with fermentation defects, so for good olive oil, care is taken to ensure the olives are perfectly ripe. The process is generally as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>The olives are ground into paste using large millstones (traditional method), hammer, blade, or disk mill (modern method).</li>
<li>If ground with millstones, the olive paste generally stays under the stones for 30 to 40 minutes. A shorter grinding process may result in a more raw paste that produces less oil and has a less ripe taste; a longer process may increase oxidation of the paste and reduce the flavor. After grinding, the olive paste is spread on fiber disks, which are stacked on top of each other in a column, then placed into the press. Pressure is then applied to the column to separate the vegetal liquid from the paste. This liquid still contains a significant amount of water. Traditionally, the oil was shed from the water by gravity (oil is less dense than water). This very slow separation process has been replaced by centrifugation, which is much faster and more thorough. The centrifuges have one exit for the (heavier) watery part and one for the oil. Olive oil should not contain significant traces of vegetal water, as this accelerates the process of organic degeneration by microorganisms. The separation in smaller oil mills is not always perfect; thus, a small watery deposit containing organic particles can be found at the bottom of oil bottles.</li>
<li>Modern grinders reduce the olives to paste in seconds. After grinding, the paste is stirred slowly for another 20 to 30 minutes in a particular container (malaxation), where the microscopic oil drops aggregate into larger drops, facilitating the mechanical extraction. The paste is then pressed by centrifugation/ the water is thereafter separated from the oil in a second centrifugation as described before.<p>The oil produced by only physical (mechanical) means as described above is called virgin oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extra virgin olive oil is virgin olive oil that satisfies specific high chemical and <a href="/wiki/Organoleptic" title="Organoleptic">organoleptic</a> criteria (low free acidity, no or very little organoleptic defects). A higher grade extra virgin olive oil is mostly dependent on favourable weather conditions; a drought during the flowering phase, for example, can result in a lower quality (virgin) oil. Olive trees produce well every couple of years, so greater harvests occur in alternate years (the year in between is when the tree yields less). However, the quality is still dependent on the weather.</p></li>
<li>Sometimes the produced oil will be filtered to eliminate remaining solid particles that may reduce the shelf life of the product. Labels may indicate the fact that the oil has <i>not</i> been filtered, suggesting a different taste. Fresh <a href="/wiki/Unfiltered_olive_oil" title="Unfiltered olive oil">unfiltered olive oil</a> usually has a slightly cloudy appearance, and is therefore sometimes called <i>cloudy olive oil</i>. This form of olive oil used to be popular only among small-scale producers but is now becoming "trendy", in line with consumers' demand for products perceived to be less processed. But generally, if not tasted or consumed soon after production, filtered olive oil should be preferred: "Some producers maintain that extra-virgin olive oils do not need filtration but also that filtration is detrimental to oil quality. This point of view should be considered erroneous and probably the result of the improper implementation of this operation. In fact, fine particles that are suspended in a virgin olive oil, even after the most effective centrifugal finishing, contain water and enzymes that may impair oil stability and ruin its sensory profile.&#160;... Filtration makes an extra-virgin olive oil more stable and also more attractive. If the suspended particles are not removed, they slowly agglomerate and flocculate, forming a deposit on the bottom of the storage containers. Such a deposit continues to be at risk of enzymatic spoilage and, in the worst case, of the development of anaerobic micro-organisms with further spoilage and hygienic risk.&#160;... It is&#160;... recommended that filtration be carried out as soon as possible after centrifugal separation and finishing."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ancient_Levant">Ancient Levant</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ancient Levant"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, three methods were used to produce different grades of olive oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The finest oil was produced from fully developed and ripe olives harvested solely from the apex of the tree,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ZAmar2015&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZAmar2015-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and lightly pressed, "for what flows from light pressure is very sweet and very thin".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geoponica1805&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoponica1805-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The remaining olives are pressed with a heavier weight,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geoponica1805&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoponica1805-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and vary in ripeness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ZAmar2015&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZAmar2015-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Inferior oil is produced from unripe olives that are stored for extended periods until they grow soft or begin to shrivel to become more fit for grinding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others are left for extended periods in pits in the ground to induce sweating and decay before they are ground.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Geoponica" title="Geoponica">Geoponica</a></i>, salt and a little <a href="/wiki/Nitre" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitre">nitre</a> are added when oil is stored.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geoponica1805&#95;47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoponica1805-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In countries of the levant, a sharp-tasting green oil was sometimes extracted from unripe olives, known in medieval times as <i>anpeqinon</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This doesn&#39;t look right: medieval Greek didn&#39;t have qoppa, Latin always followed q with u, no ghits other than this page (July 2024)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ὀμφάκιον, ὀμφάχινον</span>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">زيت الأنفاق</span>), being a corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> words <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">oleum omphacium</i></span> and used in cuisine and in medicine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RFrankel1994&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFrankel1994-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, this oil is called "virgin oil" in English.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RFrankel1994&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFrankel1994-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pomace_handling">Pomace handling</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pomace handling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The remaining semi-solid waste, called <a href="/wiki/Pomace" title="Pomace">pomace</a>, retains a small quantity (about 5–10%) of oil that cannot be extracted by further pressing, but only with chemical solvents. This is done in specialized chemical plants, not in the oil mills. The resulting oil is named <a href="/wiki/Pomace_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Pomace oil">pomace oil</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Berbel&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berbel-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even in the ancient Roman period, pomace was commonly used as a domestic and industrial fuel source, burning at a high temperature for longer and with less smoke than charcoal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Handling of olive waste is an environmental challenge because the <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a>, which amounts to millions of tons (billions of liters) annually in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, has low <a href="/wiki/Biodegradability" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodegradability">biodegradability</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Toxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic">toxic</a> to plants, and cannot be processed through conventional water treatment systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Berbel&#95;53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berbel-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditionally, olive pomace would be used as <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a> or developed as a possible <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>, although these uses introduce concern due to chemicals present in the pomace.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Berbel&#95;53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berbel-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A process called "valorization" of olive pomace is under research and development, consisting of additional processing to obtain value-added <a href="/wiki/Byproduct" class="mw-redirect" title="Byproduct">byproducts</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">animal feed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additives</a> for human products, and <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">phenolic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Extract" title="Extract">extracts</a> for potential human use.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Berbel&#95;53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berbel-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Global_market">Global market</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Global market"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
