<p>In 2022, world production of olive oil was 2.7 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by Spain with 24% of the total (table). Other major producers were Italy, Greece, Turkey, and Tunisia (table). 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Villacarrillo" title="Villacarrillo">Villacarrillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Ja%C3%A9n_(Spain)" title="Province of Jaén (Spain)">Jaén</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andaluc%C3%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Andalucía">Andalucía</a>, Spain, is a centre of olive oil production. 75% of Spain's olive oil production derives from the region of <a href="/wiki/Andaluc%C3%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Andalucía">Andalucía</a>, particularly within <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Ja%C3%A9n_(Spain)" title="Province of Jaén (Spain)">Jaén province</a> which produces 70% of the olive oil in Spain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Villacarrillo&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villacarrillo-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The world's largest olive oil <a href="/wiki/Mill_(grinding)" title="Mill (grinding)">mill</a> (<i>almazara</i>, in Spanish), capable of processing 2,500 tonnes of olives per day, is in the town of <a href="/wiki/Villacarrillo" title="Villacarrillo">Villacarrillo</a>, Jaén.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Villacarrillo&#95;55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villacarrillo-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Italian major producers are the regions of <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a> and, above all, <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a>. Many PDO and PGI extra-virgin olive oils are produced in these regions. Extra-virgin olive oil is also produced in <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in cities including <a href="/wiki/Lucca" title="Lucca">Lucca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>, which are also included in the association of Città dell'Olio.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Italy imports about 65% of Spanish olive oil exports.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Global_consumption">Global consumption</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Global consumption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Greece has the highest per capita consumption of olive oil worldwide, around 24&#160;liters per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeAndreis&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeAndreis-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consumption in Spain is 15&#160;liters; Italy 13&#160;liters;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeAndreis&#95;59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeAndreis-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Syria, around 3&#160;liters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SyriaConsumption&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SyriaConsumption-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Canada consumes 1.5&#160;liters and the US 1&#160;liter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeAndreis&#95;59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeAndreis-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Regulation">Regulation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_regulation_and_adulteration" title="Olive oil regulation and adulteration">Olive oil regulation and adulteration</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Olive_Council" title="International Olive Council">International Olive Council</a> (IOC) is an intergovernmental organisation of states that produce olives or products derived from olives, such as olive oil. The IOC officially governs 95% of international production and influences the rest. The EU regulates the use of different <a href="/wiki/Protected_designation_of_origin" title="Protected designation of origin">protected designation of origin</a> labels for olive oils.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The United States is not a member of the IOC and is not subject to its authority, but on October 25, 2010, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture">U.S. Department of Agriculture</a> adopted new voluntary olive oil grading standards that closely parallel those of the IOC, with some adjustments for the characteristics of olives grown in the U.S.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> U.S. Customs regulations on "country of origin" state that if a non-origin nation is shown on the label, then the real origin must be shown on the same side of the label in letters of comparable size, so as not to mislead the consumer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yet most major U.S. brands continue to put "imported from Italy" on the front label in large letters and other origins on the back in very small print.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-false&#95;olive&#95;oil&#95;labeling&#95;pretending&#95;to&#95;be&#95;made&#95;in&#95;Italy&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-false_olive_oil_labeling_pretending_to_be_made_in_Italy-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "In fact, olive oil labeled 'Italian' often comes from Turkey, Tunisia, Morocco, Spain, and Greece."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This makes it unclear what percentage of the olive oil is really of Italian origin.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Commercial_grades">Commercial grades</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Commercial grades"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Italian_olive_oil_2007.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Italian_olive_oil_2007.jpg/120px-Italian_olive_oil_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="357" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Italian_olive_oil_2007.jpg/250px-Italian_olive_oil_2007.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="858" data-file-height="2781" /></a><figcaption>A bottle of Italian olive oil</figcaption></figure>
<p>All production begins by crushing or pressing the olive fruit to transform it into olive paste. This paste is <a href="/wiki/Malaxation" title="Malaxation">malaxed</a> (slowly churned or mixed) to allow the microscopic oil droplets to agglomerate. The oil is then separated from the watery matter and fruit pulp with the use of a press (traditional method) or <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge" title="Centrifuge">centrifugation</a> (modern method). The residue that remains after pressing or centrifugation can also produce a small amount of oil, called <a href="/wiki/Olive_mill_pomace" title="Olive mill pomace">pomace</a>.
</p><p>One parameter used to characterise an oil is its <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_acidity" title="Olive oil acidity">acidity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this context, "acidity" is not <a href="/wiki/Acid#Acid_strength" title="Acid">chemical acidity</a> in the sense of <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>, but the percent (measured by weight) of <a href="/wiki/Free_fatty_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Free fatty acids">free</a> <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>. Olive oil acidity is a measure of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of the oil's triglycerides: as the oil degrades and becomes oxidized, more fatty acids are freed from the <a href="/wiki/Glyceride" title="Glyceride">glycerides</a>, increasing the level of free acidity and thereby increasing <a href="/wiki/Rancidification#Hydrolytic" title="Rancidification">hydrolytic rancidity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rancidity not only impacts the taste and color but also its nutritional value.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another measure of the oil's chemical degradation is the <a href="/wiki/Organic_peroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic peroxide">peroxide value</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which measures the degree to which the oil is <a href="/wiki/Oxidized" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidized">oxidized</a> by <a href="/wiki/Free_radicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Free radicals">free radicals</a>, leading to <i>oxidative</i> rancidity. <a href="/wiki/Phenolic_acid" title="Phenolic acid">Phenolic acids</a> present in olive oil also add acidic <a href="/wiki/Sensory_perception" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensory perception">sensory</a> properties to aroma and flavor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bendini&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bendini-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The grades of oil extracted from the olive fruit can be classified as:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Virgin</i> means the oil was produced by the use of mechanical means only, with no chemical treatment. The term <i>virgin oil</i> with reference to production method includes all grades of virgin olive oil, including <i>extra virgin</i>, <i>virgin</i>, <i>ordinary virgin</i> and <i>Lampante virgin</i> olive oil products, depending on quality (see below).</li>
<li><i>Lampante virgin oil</i><span class="anchor" id="Lampante"></span> is olive oil extracted by virgin (mechanical) methods but not suitable for human consumption without further refining; "lampante" is the attributive form of "lampa", the Italian word for "lamp", referring to the use of such oil in <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamps</a>. Lampante virgin oil can be used for industrial purposes, or refined (see below) to make it edible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>Refined olive oil</i> is olive oil obtained from any grade of virgin olive oil by refining methods that do not lead to alterations in the initial glyceridic structure. The refining process removes color, odor, and flavour from the olive oil, and leaves behind a very pure form of olive oil that is tasteless, colorless, odorless, and extremely low in free fatty acids. Olive oils sold as the grades <i>extra virgin olive oil</i> and <i>virgin olive oil</i> therefore cannot contain any refined oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>Crude olive pomace oil</i> is the oil obtained by treating olive pomace (the leftover paste after the pressing of olives for virgin olive oils) with solvents or other physical treatments, to the exclusion of oils obtained by re-esterification processes and of any mixture with other kinds of oils. It is then further refined into <i>refined olive pomace oil</i> and once re-blended with virgin olive oils for taste, is then known as <i>olive pomace oil</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Olio_prodotto_in_Liguria.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Olio_prodotto_in_Liguria.JPG/250px-Olio_prodotto_in_Liguria.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="338" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Olio_prodotto_in_Liguria.JPG/500px-Olio_prodotto_in_Liguria.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="1826" data-file-height="2468" /></a><figcaption>Italian label for "extra vergine" olive oil</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="International_Olive_Council">International Olive Council</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: International Olive Council"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In countries that adhere to the standards of the <a href="/wiki/International_Olive_Council" title="International Olive Council">International Olive Council</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ioc&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as in Australia, and under the voluntary <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> labeling standards in the United States:
</p><p><b>Extra virgin olive oil</b> is the highest grade of virgin olive oil derived by <a href="/wiki/Cold_extraction" class="mw-redirect" title="Cold extraction">cold mechanical extraction</a> without use of <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a> or refining methods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It contains no more than 0.8% <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_acidity" title="Olive oil acidity">free acidity</a>, and is judged to have a superior taste, having some fruitiness and no defined sensory defects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Prosodol&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prosodol-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extra virgin olive oil accounts for less than 10% of oil in many producing countries; the percentage is far higher in some Mediterranean countries.
</p><p>The International Olive Council requires the median of the fruity attribute to be higher than zero for a given olive oil in order to meet the criteria of extra virgin olive oil classification.
</p><p><b>Virgin olive oil</b> is a lesser grade of virgin oil, with <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_acidity" title="Olive oil acidity">free acidity</a> of up to 2.0%, and is judged to have a good taste, but may include some sensory defects.
</p><p><b>Refined olive oil</b> is virgin oil refined using charcoal and other chemical and physical filters, methods which do not alter the glyceridic structure. It has a free acidity, expressed as oleic acid, of not more than 0.3 grams per 100 grams (0.3%), and its other characteristics correspond to those fixed for this category in this standard. It is obtained by refining virgin oils to eliminate high acidity or organoleptic defects. Oils labeled as <i>pure olive oil</i> or <i>olive oil</i> are primarily refined olive oil, with a small addition of virgin oil for taste.
</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/Olive_pomace_oil" title="Olive pomace oil">Olive pomace oil</a></b> is refined pomace olive oil, often blended with some virgin oil. It is fit for consumption, but may not be described simply as <i>olive oil</i>. It has a more neutral flavor than pure or virgin olive oil, making it less desirable to users concerned with flavor; however, it has the same fat composition as regular olive oil, giving it the same health benefits. It also has a high smoke point, and consequently is widely used in restaurants and home cooking in some countries.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="United_States">United States</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The United States is not a member of the IOC and does not implement its grades, but on October 25, 2010, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> (USDA) established Standards for Grades of Olive Oil and Olive-Pomace Oil which closely parallel the IOC standards:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-usda2&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda2-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-usda&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>U.S. extra virgin olive oil</b> for oil with excellent flavor and odor and <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil_acidity" title="Olive oil acidity">free fatty acid content</a> of not more than 0.8&#160;g per 100&#160;g (0.8%);</li>
<li><b>U.S. virgin olive oil</b> for oil with reasonably good flavor and odor and free fatty acid content of not more than 2&#160;g per 100&#160;g (2%);</li>
<li><b>U.S. virgin olive oil Not Fit For Human Consumption Without Further Processing</b> is a virgin (mechanically-extracted) olive oil of poor flavor and odor, equivalent to the IOC's <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">lampante</i></span> oil;</li>
<li><b>U.S. olive oil</b> is a mixture of virgin and refined oils;</li>
<li><b>U.S. refined olive oil</b> is an oil made from refined oils with some restrictions on the processing.</li></ul>
<p>These grades are voluntary. Certification is available, for a fee, from the USDA.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-usda&#95;77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, California adopted a set of olive oil standards for olive oil made from California-grown olives. The California Department of Food and Agriculture Grade and Labeling Standards for Olive Oil, Refined-Olive Oil, and Olive-Pomace Oil are mandatory for producers of more than 5,000 <a href="/wiki/US_gallon" class="mw-redirect" title="US gallon">gallons</a> of California olive oil. This joins other official state, federal, and international olive oil standards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Several olive producer associations, such as the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Olive_Oil_Association" title="North American Olive Oil Association">North American Olive Oil Association</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=California_Olive_Oil_Council&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="California Olive Oil Council (page does not exist)">California Olive Oil Council</a>, also offer grading and certification within the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Oleologist Nicholas Coleman suggests that the California Olive Oil Council certification is the most stringent of the voluntary grading schemes in the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Country of origin can be established by one or two-letter country codes printed on the bottle or label. Country codes include I=Italy, GR=Greece, E=Spain, TU=Tunisia, MA=Morocco, CL=Chile, AG=Argentina, AU=Australia.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Label_wording">Label wording</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Label wording"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>Different names for olive oil indicate the degree of processing the oil has undergone as well as the quality of the oil. Extra virgin olive oil is the highest grade available, followed by virgin olive oil. The word "virgin" indicates that the olives have been pressed to extract the oil; no heat or chemicals have been used during the extraction process, and the oil is pure and unrefined. Virgin olive oils contain the highest levels of <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>, antioxidants that have been linked with better health.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>Olive Oil</i>, which is sometimes denoted as being "Made from refined and virgin olive oils" is a blend of refined olive oil with a virgin grade of olive oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Pure</i>, <i>Classic</i>, <i>Light</i> and <i>Extra-Light</i> are terms introduced by manufacturers in countries that are non-traditional consumers of olive oil for these products to indicate both their composition of being only 100% olive oil, and also the varying strength of taste to consumers. Contrary to a common consumer belief, they do not have fewer calories than extra virgin oil, as implied by the names.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bogle&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bogle-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>Cold pressed</i> or <i>Cold extraction</i> means "that the oil was not heated over a certain temperature (usually 27&#160;°C (80&#160;°F)) during processing, thus retaining more nutrients and undergoing less degradation".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pomace&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pomace-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The difference between <i>Cold Extraction</i> and <i>Cold Pressed</i> is regulated in Europe, where the use of a centrifuge, the modern method of extraction for large quantities, must be labelled as <i>Cold Extracted</i>, while only a physically pressed olive oil may be labelled as <i>Cold Pressed</i>. In many parts of the world, such as Australia, producers using centrifugal extraction still label their products as <i>Cold Pressed</i>.</li>
<li><i>First cold pressed</i> means "that the fruit of the olive was crushed exactly one time – i.e., the <i>first press.</i> The <i>cold</i> refers to the temperature range of the fruit at the time it is crushed".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Calabria (Italy) the olives are collected in October. In regions like Tuscany or Liguria, the olives collected in November and ground, often at night, are too cold to be processed efficiently without heating. The paste is regularly heated above the environmental temperatures, which may be as low as 10–15&#160;°C, to extract the oil efficiently with only physical means. Olives pressed in warm regions like Southern Italy or Northern Africa may be pressed at significantly higher temperatures, although not heated. While it is important that the pressing temperatures be as low as possible (generally below 25&#160;°C) there is no international reliable definition of "cold pressed".<br />Furthermore, there is no "second" press of virgin oil, so the term "first press" means only that the oil was produced in a press vs. other possible methods.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Protected_designation_of_origin" title="Protected designation of origin">Protected designation of origin</a></i> (PDO) and <i><a href="/wiki/Protected_geographical_indication" class="mw-redirect" title="Protected geographical indication">protected geographical indication</a></i> (PGI) refer to olive oils with "exceptional properties and quality derived from their place of origin as well as from the way of their production".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The label may indicate that the oil was bottled or packed in a stated country. This does not necessarily mean that the oil was produced there. The origin of the oil may sometimes be marked elsewhere on the label; it may be a mixture of oils from multiple countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-false&#95;olive&#95;oil&#95;labeling&#95;pretending&#95;to&#95;be&#95;made&#95;in&#95;Italy&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-false_olive_oil_labeling_pretending_to_be_made_in_Italy-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The U.S. Food and Drug Administration permitted a claim on olive oil labels stating: "Limited and not conclusive scientific evidence suggests that eating about two tablespoons (23&#160;g) of olive oil daily may reduce the risk of coronary heart disease."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Drummond&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drummond-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Adulteration">Adulteration</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Adulteration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There have been allegations, particularly in Italy and Spain, that regulation can be sometimes lax and corrupt.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Major shippers are claimed to routinely adulterate olive oil so that only about 40% of olive oil sold as "extra virgin" in Italy actually meets the specification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mueller&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some cases, <a href="/wiki/Colza_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Colza oil">colza oil</a> (extracted from <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a>) with added color and flavor has been labeled and sold as olive oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eubusiness&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eubusiness-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This extensive fraud prompted the Italian government to mandate a new labeling law in 2007 for companies selling olive oil, under which every bottle of Italian olive oil would have to declare the farm and press on which it was produced, as well as display a precise breakdown of the oils used, for blended oils.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-telegraph&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In February 2008, however, EU officials took issue with the new law, stating that under EU rules such labeling should be voluntary rather than compulsory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eubusiness&#95;90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eubusiness-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Under EU rules, olive oil may be sold as Italian even if it only contains a small amount of Italian oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-telegraph&#95;91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Extra-virgin olive oil has strict requirements and is checked for "sensory defects" that include: rancid, fusty, musty, winey (vinegary), and muddy sediment. These defects can occur for different reasons. The most common are:
</p>
<ul><li>Raw material (olives) infected or battered</li>
<li>Inadequate harvest, with contact between the olives and soil<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In March 2008, 400 Italian police officers conducted Operation Golden Oil, arresting 23 people and confiscating 85 farms after an investigation revealed a large-scale scheme to relabel oils from other Mediterranean nations as Italian.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In April 2008, another operation impounded seven olive oil plants and arrested 40 people in nine provinces of northern and southern Italy for adding <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_oil" title="Sunflower oil">sunflower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soya bean oil</a> and selling it as extra virgin olive oil, both in Italy and abroad; 25,000 liters of the fake oil were seized and prevented from being exported.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On March 15, 2011, the prosecutor's office in Florence, Italy, working in conjunction with the forestry department, indicted two managers and an officer of Carapelli, one of the brands of the Spanish company Grupo SOS (which recently changed its name to Deoleo). The charges involved falsified documents and food fraud. Carapelli lawyer Neri Pinucci said the company was not worried about the charges and that "the case is based on an irregularity in the documents."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2012, Spanish authorities investigated an international olive oil fraud in which palm, avocado, sunflower, and other cheaper oils were passed off as Italian olive oil. Police said the oils were blended in an industrial biodiesel plant and adulterated in a way to hide markers that would have revealed their true nature. The oils were not toxic and posed no health risk, according to a statement by the <a href="/wiki/Guardia_Civil" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardia Civil">Guardia Civil</a>. Nineteen people were arrested following the year-long joint probe by the police and Spanish tax authorities, as part of  Operation Lucerna.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Using tiny print to state the origin of blended oil is used as a legal loophole by manufacturers of adulterated and mixed olive oil.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Journalist Tom Mueller has investigated crime and adulteration in the olive oil business, publishing the article "Slippery Business" in <i>New Yorker</i> magazine,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mueller&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> followed by the 2011 book <i><a href="/wiki/Extra_Virginity" title="Extra Virginity">Extra Virginity</a></i>. On 3 January 2016 <a href="/wiki/Bill_Whitaker_(journalist)" title="Bill Whitaker (journalist)">Bill Whitaker</a> presented a program on CBS News including interviews with Mueller and with Italian authorities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was reported that in the previous month, 5,000 tons of adulterated olive oil had been sold in Italy, and that <a href="/wiki/Organised_crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Organised crime">organised crime</a> was heavily involved—the term "Agrimafia" was used. The point was made by Mueller that the profit margin on adulterated olive oil was three times that on the illegal narcotic drug <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>. He said that over 50% of olive oil sold in Italy was adulterated, as was 75–80% of that sold in the US. Whitaker reported that three samples of "extra virgin olive oil" had been bought in a US supermarket and tested; two of the three samples did not meet the required standard, and one of them—from a top-selling US brand—was exceptionally poor.
</p><p>In early February 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a> police arrested 33 suspects in the Calabrian mafia's <a href="/wiki/Piromalli_%27ndrina" title="Piromalli &#39;ndrina">Piromalli 'ndrina</a> (<a href="/wiki/%27Ndrangheta" title="&#39;Ndrangheta">'Ndrangheta</a>), which was allegedly exporting fake extra virgin olive oil to the U.S.; the product was actually inexpensive olive pomace oil fraudulently labeled.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Less than a year earlier, the American television program <i><a href="/wiki/60_Minutes" title="60 Minutes">60 Minutes</a></i> had warned that "the olive oil business has been corrupted by the Mafia" and that "Agromafia" was a 16 billion <a href="/wiki/US_dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="US dollar">dollar</a> per year enterprise. A Carabinieri investigator interviewed on the program said that "olive oil fraud has gone on for the better part of four millennia" but today, it's particularly "easy for the bad guys to either introduce adulterated olive oils or mix in lower quality olive oils with extra-virgin olive oil".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Weeks later, a report by <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine stated that "it's reliably reported that 80% of the Italian olive oil on the [US] market is fraudulent" and that "a massive olive oil scandal is being uncovered in Southern Italy (Puglia, Umbria and Campania)".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Quality_control_and_fraud">Quality control and fraud</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Quality control and fraud"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In July 2024, the European Union reported a significant increase in olive oil fraud and mislabeling cases. The European Commission's annual report on food fraud revealed that olive oil remained one of the most frequently adulterated food products, with incidents reaching a record high.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2024Goodier&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2024Goodier-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Mislabeling_and_adulteration">Mislabeling and adulteration</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mislabeling and adulteration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The most common forms of olive oil fraud include:
</p>
<ol><li>Mislabeling lower-grade oils as extra virgin olive oil</li>
<li>Diluting olive oil with cheaper vegetable oils</li>
<li>Falsely claiming EU origin for non-EU oils</li></ol>
<p>These fraudulent practices not only deceive consumers but also undermine the reputation of legitimate producers, particularly those from traditional olive-growing regions in the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2024Goodier&#95;103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2024Goodier-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="EU_response">EU response</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: EU response"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>To combat the rising fraud cases, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> has implemented several measures:
</p>
<ul><li>Increased inspections and testing of olive oil shipments</li>
<li>Enhanced traceability requirements for producers and distributors</li>
<li>Stricter penalties for companies found guilty of fraud</li>
<li>Improved cooperation between member states' food safety authorities</li></ul>
<p>The EU has also launched a public awareness campaign to educate consumers about olive oil quality and how to identify authentic products.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2024Goodier&#95;103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2024Goodier-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Impact_on_the_industry">Impact on the industry</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Impact on the industry"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The surge in fraud cases has led to:
</p>
<ul><li>Higher production costs for honest producers due to increased compliance requirements</li>
<li>Potential market share loss for authentic extra virgin olive oil as cheaper, adulterated products flood the market</li>
<li>Growing consumer skepticism about olive oil quality claims</li></ul>
<p>Industry experts emphasize the importance of supporting reputable producers and urge consumers to be more vigilant when purchasing olive oil, particularly when prices seem unusually low for claimed quality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2024Goodier&#95;103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2024Goodier-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Constituents">Constituents</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Constituents"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fat_structural_formulae_V3.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Fat_structural_formulae_V3.svg/250px-Fat_structural_formulae_V3.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Fat_structural_formulae_V3.svg/500px-Fat_structural_formulae_V3.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="306" data-file-height="218" /></a><figcaption>General chemical structure of food fats (<a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglyceride</a>). R<sup>1</sup>, R<sup>2</sup>, and R<sup>3</sup> are <a href="/wiki/Alkyl_group" title="Alkyl group">alkyl groups</a> (approx. 20%) or alkenyl groups (approx. 80%).</figcaption></figure>
<p>Olive oil is composed mainly of the mixed <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglyceride</a> esters of <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic acid</a> and of other <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-oos&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oos-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beltran2004&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beltran2004-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> along with traces of <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> (up to 0.7%) and <a href="/wiki/Sterol" title="Sterol">sterols</a> (about 0.2% <a href="/wiki/Phytosterol" title="Phytosterol">phytosterol</a> and tocosterols). The composition varies by cultivar, region, altitude, time of harvest, and extraction process.
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phenolic_composition">Phenolic composition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Phenolic composition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Olive oil contains traces of <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a> (about 0.5%), such as <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tyrosol" title="Tyrosol">tyrosol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxytyrosol" title="Hydroxytyrosol">hydroxytyrosol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oleocanthal" title="Oleocanthal">oleocanthal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oleuropein" title="Oleuropein">oleuropein</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bendini&#95;71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bendini-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-phenolics&#95;135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phenolics-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which give olive oil a slightly <a href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitter (taste)">bitter taste</a> and characteristic aroma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Olive oil contains numerous polyphenols, among which are <a href="/wiki/Elenolic_acid" title="Elenolic acid">elenolic acid</a>, a marker for maturation of olives,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bendini&#95;71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bendini-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and alpha-<a href="/wiki/Tocopherol" title="Tocopherol">tocopherol</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">vitamin E</a> compound.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wagner&#95;et&#95;al,&#95;2004&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wagner_et_al,_2004-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oleuropein" title="Oleuropein">Oleuropein</a>, together with other closely related compounds such as <a href="/wiki/10-hydroxyoleuropein" class="mw-redirect" title="10-hydroxyoleuropein">10-hydroxyoleuropein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligstroside" title="Ligstroside">ligstroside</a> and <a href="/wiki/10-hydroxyligstroside" class="mw-redirect" title="10-hydroxyligstroside">10-hydroxyligstroside</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Tyrosol" title="Tyrosol">tyrosol</a> <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> of elenolic acid. Other phenolic constituents include <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lignan" title="Lignan">lignans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pinoresinol" title="Pinoresinol">pinoresinol</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2026)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Nutrition">Nutrition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nutrition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/food-details/171413/nutrients">Full Link to USDA Database entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0; color:inherit;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FDADailyValues&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Olive oil is 100% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>, containing no <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a> or water (table).
</p><p>In a reference amount of 100 grams (3.5&#160;oz), olive oil supplies 884 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">kcals</a> of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>, and is a rich source (20% or more of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>, DV) of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">vitamin E</a> (96% DV) and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a> (57% DV) (table). 
</p><p>One <a href="/wiki/Tablespoon" title="Tablespoon">tablespoon</a> (13.5&#160;g) of olive oil supplies 500&#160;kJ (119&#160;kcal) of food energy and contains 13.5 g of fat, including 9.9 g of <a href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">monounsaturated fat</a> (mainly as <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>), 1.4 g of <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fat" title="Polyunsaturated fat">polyunsaturated fat</a> (mainly as <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a>), and 1.9 g of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> (mainly as <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic acid</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Potential_health_effects">Potential health effects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Olive_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Potential health effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_Olives.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Egyptian_Olives.jpg/250px-Egyptian_Olives.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="195" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Egyptian_Olives.jpg/500px-Egyptian_Olives.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2872" data-file-height="2236" /></a><figcaption>Egyptian olives</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean diet</a></div>
<p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/FDA" class="mw-redirect" title="FDA">FDA</a> allows producers of olive oil to place the following qualified <a href="/wiki/Health_claim" title="Health claim">health claim</a> on <a href="/wiki/Private_label" title="Private label">product labels</a>:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Limited and not conclusive scientific evidence suggests that eating about 2 tbsp. (23 g) of olive oil daily may reduce the risk of coronary heart disease due to the monounsaturated fat in olive oil. To achieve this possible benefit, olive oil is to replace a similar amount of saturated fat and not increase the overall number of calories consumed in a day.</p></blockquote>
<p>In a review by the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) in 2011, health claims on olive oil were approved for protection by its <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a> against <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> of blood lipids,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EFSApolyphenols2011&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSApolyphenols2011-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and for maintenance of normal blood <a href="/wiki/LDL-cholesterol" class="mw-redirect" title="LDL-cholesterol">LDL-cholesterol</a> levels by replacing saturated fats in the diet with <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EFSAoleicacid2011&#95;145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSAoleicacid2011-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (See also: Commission Regulation (EU) 432/2012 of 16 May 2012).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-OfficialJournalEuropeanUnion2012&#95;146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OfficialJournalEuropeanUnion2012-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite its approval, the EFSA has noted that a definitive <a href="/wiki/Cause-and-effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Cause-and-effect">cause-and-effect</a> relationship has not been adequately established for consumption of olive oil and maintaining normal (fasting) blood concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Triglycerides" class="mw-redirect" title="Triglycerides">triglycerides</a>, normal blood <a href="/wiki/HDL-cholesterol" class="mw-redirect" title="HDL-cholesterol">HDL-cholesterol</a> concentrations, and normal <a href="/wiki/Blood_glucose" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood glucose">blood glucose</a> concentrations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EFSA&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 meta-analysis concluded that increased consumption of olive oil was associated with reduced risk of all-cause mortality, cardiovascular events and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, while monounsaturated fatty acids of mixed animal and plant origin showed no significant effects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schw&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schw-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another meta-analysis in 2018 found high-polyphenol olive oil intake was associated with improved measures of total cholesterol, HDL cholesterol, <a href="/wiki/Malondialdehyde" title="Malondialdehyde">malondialdehyde</a>, and oxidized LDL when compared to low-polyphenol olive oils, although it recommended longer studies, and more investigation of non-Mediterranean populations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite&#95;note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFCastleden2005" class="citation book cs1">Castleden, Rodney (2005). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Kfi0dAlfJaoC"><i>The Mycenaeans</i></a>. London and New York: Routledge. p.&#160;107. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-36336-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-36336-5"><bdi>978-0-415-36336-5</bdi></a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20230115132907/https://books.google.com/books?id=Kfi0dAlfJaoC">Archived</a> from the original on January 15, 2023<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">April 3,</span> 2016</span>. <q>Huge quantities of olive oil were produced and it must have been a major source of wealth. The simple fact that southern Greece is far more suitable climatically for olive production may explain why the Mycenaean civilization made far greater advances in the south than in the north. The oil had a variety of uses, in cooking, as a dressing, as soap, as lamp oil, and as a base for manufacturing unguents.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Mycenaeans&amp;rft.place=London+and+New+York&amp;rft.pages=107&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-36336-5&amp;rft.aulast=Castleden&amp;rft.aufirst=Rodney&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DKfi0dAlfJaoC&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOlive+oil" class="Z3988"></span></span>
